       This article is about the
       <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">
        canonical
       </a>
       book of the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">
        Hebrew Bible
       </a>

       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samaritan" title="Samaritan">
        Samaritan
       </a>
       version, see
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Joshua_(Samaritan)" title="Book of Joshua (Samaritan)">
        Book of Joshua (Samaritan)
       </a>
       .

         </a>

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Tanakh_OT" title="Template:Tanakh OT">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Tanakh_OT" title="Template talk:Tanakh OT">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Tanakh_OT&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Book of Joshua
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        ספר יהושע
       </span>
       ‎
       <i>
        Sefer Yĕhôshúa
       </i>
       ) is the sixth book in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">
        Hebrew Bible
       </a>
       (the Christian
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
        Old Testament
       </a>
       ) and the first book of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuteronomistic_history" title="Deuteronomistic history">
        Deuteronomistic history
       </a>
       , the story of Israel from the conquest of Canaan to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_exile" title="Babylonian exile">
        Babylonian exile
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNutt1999_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McNutt1999-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         42
        </span>
       </sup>
       It tells of the campaigns of the
       <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
        Israelites
       </a>
       in central, southern and northern
       <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
        Canaan
       </a>
       , the destruction of their enemies, and the division of the land among the
       <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Tribes_of_Israel" title="Twelve Tribes of Israel">
        Twelve Tribes
       </a>
       , framed by two set-piece speeches, the first by God commanding the conquest of the land, and, at the end, the last by
       <a href="/wiki/Joshua" title="Joshua">
        Joshua
       </a>
       warning of the need for faithful observance of the Law (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
         torah
        </a>
       </i>
       ) revealed to
       <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
        Moses
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-achb_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-achb-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Almost all scholars agree that the Book of Joshua holds little historical value for early Israel and most likely reflects a much later period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Killebrew_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Killebrew-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         152
        </span>
       </sup>
       Although
       <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">
        Rabbinic
       </a>
       tradition holds that the book was written by Joshua, it is probable that it was written by multiple authors and editors far removed from the times it depicts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Creach_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Creach-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         9-10
        </span>
       </sup>
       The earliest parts of the book are possibly chapters 2–11, the story of the conquest; these chapters were later incorporated into an early form of Joshua written late in the reign of king
       <a href="/wiki/Josiah" title="Josiah">
        Josiah
       </a>
       (reigned 640–609 BCE), but the book was not completed until after the fall of
       <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
        Jerusalem
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">
        Neo-Babylonian Empire
       </a>
       in 586 BCE, and possibly not until after the return from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_exile" title="Babylonian exile">
        Babylonian exile
       </a>
       in 539 BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Creach_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Creach-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         10–11
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contents">
        Contents
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Contents">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="411" data-file-width="535" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg/220px-Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg/330px-Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg/440px-Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dore_joshua_crossing.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Joshua and the Israelites crossing the Jordan
         </i>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave Doré">
          Gustave Doré
         </a>
         )
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">
        Structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       I. Transfer of leadership to Joshua (1:1–18)
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        A. God's commission to Joshua (1:1–9)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        B. Joshua's instructions to the people (1:10–18)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       II. Entrance into and conquest of Canaan (2:1–5:15)
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        A. Entry into Canaan
        <dl>
         <dd>
          1.Reconnaissance of
          <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">
           Jericho
          </a>
          (2:1–24)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          2. Crossing the River Jordan (3:1–17)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          3. Establishing a foothold at
          <a href="/wiki/Gilgal" title="Gilgal">
           Gilgal
          </a>
          (4:1–5:1)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          4.
          <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">
           Circumcision
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">
           Passover
          </a>
          (5:2–15)
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        B. Victory over Canaan (6:1–12:24)
        <dl>
         <dd>
          1. Destruction of Jericho (6)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          2. Failure and success at
          <a href="/wiki/Ai_(Canaan)" title="Ai (Canaan)">
           Ai
          </a>
          (7:1–8:29)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          3. Renewal of the covenant at
          <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ebal" title="Mount Ebal">
           Mount Ebal
          </a>
          (8:30–35)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          4. Other campaigns in central Canaan. The Gibeonite Deception (9:1–27)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          5. Campaigns in southern Canaan (10:1–43)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          6. Campaigns in northern Canaan (11:1–15)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          7. Summary of lands conquered (11:16-23)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          8. Summary list of defeated kings (12:1–24)
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       III. Division of the land among the tribes (13:1–22:34)
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        A. God's instructions to Joshua (13:1–7)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        B. Tribal allotments (13:8–19:51)
        <dl>
         <dd>
          1. Eastern tribes (13:8–33)
         </dd>
         <dd>
          2. Western tribes (14:1–19:51)
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        C. Cities of refuge and levitical cities (20:1–21:42)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        D. Summary of conquest (21:43–45)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        E. De-commissioning of the eastern tribes (22:1–34)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       IV. Conclusion (23:1–24:33)
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        A. Joshua's farewell address (23:1–16)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        B. Covenant at
        <a href="/wiki/Shechem" title="Shechem">
         Shechem
        </a>
        (24:1–28)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        C. Deaths of Joshua and
        <a href="/wiki/Eleazar" title="Eleazar">
         Eleazar
        </a>
        ; burial of Joseph's bones (24:29–33)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-achb_2-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-achb-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_narrative">
        Biblical narrative
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Biblical narrative">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span id="God.27s_commission_to_Joshua_.28chapter_1.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="God's_commission_to_Joshua_(chapter_1)">
        God's commission to Joshua (chapter 1)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: God's commission to Joshua (chapter 1)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Chapter 1 commences "after the death of
       <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
        Moses
       </a>
       " (Joshua 1:1) and presents the first of three important moments in Joshua marked with major speeches and reflections by the main characters; here first
       <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
        God
       </a>
       , and then
       <a href="/wiki/Joshua" title="Joshua">
        Joshua
       </a>
       , make speeches about the goal of conquest of the
       <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">
        Promised Land
       </a>
       ; in chapter 12, the narrator looks back on the conquest; and in chapter 23 Joshua gives a speech about what must be done if
       <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
        Israel
       </a>
       is to live in peace in the land.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DePury_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DePury-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         49
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       God commissions Joshua to take possession of the land and warns him to keep faith with the Covenant. God's speech foreshadows the major themes of the book: the crossing of the
       <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">
        Jordan River
       </a>
       and conquest of the land, its distribution, and the imperative need for obedience to the Law; Joshua's own immediate obedience is seen in his speeches to the Israelite commanders and to the
       <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_in_the_Bible" title="Transjordan in the Bible">
        Transjordanian
       </a>
       tribes, and the Transjordanians' affirmation of Joshua's leadership echoes
       <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">
        Yahweh
       </a>
       's assurances of victory.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younger_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Younger-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         175
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Entry_into_the_land_and_conquest_.28chapters_2.E2.80.9312.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Entry_into_the_land_and_conquest_(chapters_2–12)">
        Entry into the land and conquest (chapters 2–12)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Entry into the land and conquest (chapters 2–12)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div aria-labelledby="Early_Israelite_campaigns" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">
       <table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit">
        <tr>
         <th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;">
          <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
           <ul>
            <li class="nv-view">
             <a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Early_Israelite_campaigns" title="Template:Campaignbox Early Israelite campaigns">
              <abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none;" title="View this template">
               v
              </abbr>
             </a>
            </li>
            <li class="nv-talk">
             <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Early_Israelite_campaigns" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Early Israelite campaigns">
              <abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none;" title="Discuss this template">
               t
              </abbr>
             </a>
            </li>
            <li class="nv-edit">
             <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Early_Israelite_campaigns&amp;action=edit">
              <abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none;" title="Edit this template">
               e
              </abbr>
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </div>
          <div id="Early_Israelite_campaigns" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em">
           <span style="line-height:1.6em">
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Israelite_campaigns" title="Early Israelite campaigns">
             <span class="wrap">
              Early Israelite campaigns
             </span>
            </a>
           </span>
          </div>
         </th>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
          <div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
           <ul>
            <li>
             <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ai_(Bible)" title="Ai (Bible)">
              Ai
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/Tel_Hazor#Canaanite_Hazor" title="Tel Hazor">
              Hazor
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jericho" title="Battle of Jericho">
              Jericho
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/Tel_Lachish#Biblical_references" title="Tel Lachish">
              Lachish
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Waters_of_Merom" title="Battle of the Waters of Merom">
              Waters of Merom
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gibeah" title="Battle of Gibeah">
              Gibeah
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </div>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="813" data-file-width="994" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg/250px-Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg/375px-Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg/500px-Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joshua_Leading_the_Israelites_Across_the_Jordan_on_10th_of_Nisan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Joshua Leading the Israelites Across the Jordan on 10th of Nisan
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Israelites cross the
       <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">
        Jordan River
       </a>
       through the miraculous intervention of
       <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
        God
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">
        Ark of the Covenant
       </a>
       . Then they are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumcision_in_the_Bible" title="Circumcision in the Bible">
        circumcised
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Gilgal" title="Gilgal">
        Gibeath-Haaraloth
       </a>
       (translated as
       <i>
        hill of foreskins
       </i>
       ), renamed
       <a href="/wiki/Gilgal" title="Gilgal">
        Gilgal
       </a>
       in memory.
       <i>
        Gilgal
       </i>
       sounds like
       <i>
        Gallothi
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        I have removed
       </i>
       , but is more likely to translate as
       <i>
        circle of standing stones
       </i>
       . The conquest begins in Canaan with
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jericho" title="Battle of Jericho">
        Jericho
       </a>
       , followed by
       <a href="/wiki/Ai_(Canaan)" title="Ai (Canaan)">
        Ai
       </a>
       (central Canaan). After which Joshua builds an altar to Yahweh at
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ebal" title="Mount Ebal">
        Mount Ebal
       </a>
       (northern Canaan) and renews the
       <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(biblical)" title="Covenant (biblical)">
        Covenant
       </a>
       . The covenant ceremony has elements of a divine land-grant ceremony, similar to ceremonies known from
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younger_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Younger-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         180
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The narrative then switches to the south. The
       <a href="/wiki/Gibeon_(ancient_city)" title="Gibeon (ancient city)">
        Gibeonites
       </a>
       trick the Israelites into entering an alliance with them by saying that they are not
       <a href="/wiki/Canaan#Hebrew_Bible" title="Canaan">
        Canaanites
       </a>
       . This prevents the Israelites from exterminating them, but they are
       <a href="/wiki/The_Bible_and_slavery" title="The Bible and slavery">
        enslaved
       </a>
       instead. An alliance of
       <a href="/wiki/Amorites#Biblical_Amorites" title="Amorites">
        Amorite
       </a>
       kingdoms headed by the Canaanite king of
       <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
        Jerusalem
       </a>
       is defeated with
       <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">
        Yahweh
       </a>
       's miraculous help of stopping the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       , and hurling down large
       <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">
        hailstones
       </a>
       . (Joshua 10:10-14) The enemy kings were eventually hanged on trees. The
       <a href="/wiki/Deuteronomist" title="Deuteronomist">
        Deuteronomist
       </a>
       author may have used the then-recent 701 BCE campaign of the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Assyrian_kings" title="List of Assyrian kings">
        Assyrian king
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">
        Sennacherib
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
        Kingdom of Judah
       </a>
       as his model; the hanging of the captured kings is in accordance with
       <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">
        Assyrian
       </a>
       practice of the 8th century BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       With the south conquered the narrative moves to the northern campaign. A powerful multi-national (or more accurately, multi-ethnic) coalition headed by the king of
       <a href="/wiki/Tel_Hazor" title="Tel Hazor">
        Hazor
       </a>
       , the most important northern city, is defeated with Yahweh's help. Hazor itself is then captured and destroyed. Chapter 11:16–23 summarises the extent of the conquest: Joshua has taken the entire land, almost entirely through military victories, with only the Gibeonites agreeing to peaceful terms with Israel. The land then "had rest from war" (Joshua 11:23, repeated at 14:15). Chapter 12 lists the vanquished kings on both sides of the
       <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">
        Jordan River
       </a>
       : the two kings who ruled east of the Jordan who were defeated under Moses' leadership (Joshua 12:1-6; cf. Numbers 21), and the 31 kings on the west of the Jordan who were defeated under Joshua's leadership (Joshua 12:7-24). The list of the 31 kings is quasi-tabular:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         the king of
         <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
          Jerusalem
         </a>
         , one; the king of
         <a href="/wiki/Hebron" title="Hebron">
          Hebron
         </a>
         , one
        </i>
        ;
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         the king of
         <a href="/wiki/Jarmuth" title="Jarmuth">
          Jarmuth
         </a>
         , one; the king of
         <a href="/wiki/Tel_Lachish" title="Tel Lachish">
          Lachish
         </a>
         , one
        </i>
        ; (etc.) (Joshua 12:10-11).
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span id="Division_of_the_land_.28chapters_13.E2.80.9322.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Division_of_the_land_(chapters_13–22)">
        Division of the land (chapters 13–22)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Division of the land (chapters 13–22)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_(drawn_in_1320).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1272" data-file-width="1950" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_%28drawn_in_1320%29.jpg/220px-The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_%28drawn_in_1320%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_%28drawn_in_1320%29.jpg/330px-The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_%28drawn_in_1320%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_%28drawn_in_1320%29.jpg/440px-The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_%28drawn_in_1320%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_map_of_the_Holy_Land_by_Marino_Sanudo_(drawn_in_1320).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of the
         <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">
          Holy Land
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Vesconte" title="Pietro Vesconte">
          Pietro Vesconte
         </a>
         , 1321, showing the allotments of the tribes of Israel. Described by
         <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Erik_Nordenski%C3%B6ld" title="Adolf Erik Nordenskiöld">
          Adolf Erik Nordenskiöld
         </a>
         as "the first non-Ptolemaic map of a definite country"
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nordenskiöld1889_8-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Nordenskiöld1889-8">
           [8]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1676" data-file-width="2000" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg/220px-1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg/330px-1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg/440px-1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1759_map_Holy_Land_and_12_Tribes.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         1759 map of the tribal allotments of Israel
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Having described how the Israelites and Joshua have carried out the first of their God's commands, the narrative now turns to the second: to "put the people in possession of the land." Joshua is "old, advanced (or stricken) in years" by this time (Joshua 13:1).
      </p>
      <p>
       This land distribution is a "covenantal land grant":
       <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">
        Yahweh
       </a>
       , as king, is issuing each
       <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Tribes_of_Israel" title="Twelve Tribes of Israel">
        tribe
       </a>
       its territory.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younger_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Younger-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         183
        </span>
       </sup>
       The "
       <a href="/wiki/Cities_of_Refuge" title="Cities of Refuge">
        Cities of Refuge
       </a>
       " and
       <a href="/wiki/Levitical_city" title="Levitical city">
        Levitical cities
       </a>
       are attached to the end, since it is necessary for the tribes to receive their grants before they allocate parts of it to others. The Transjordanian tribes are dismissed, affirming their loyalty to Yahweh.
      </p>
      <p>
       The book reaffirms Moses' allocation of land east of the Jordan to the tribes of
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Reuben" title="Tribe of Reuben">
        Reuben
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Gad" title="Tribe of Gad">
        Gad
       </a>
       and the half-tribe of
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Manasseh" title="Tribe of Manasseh">
        Manasseh
       </a>
       (Joshua 13:8-32; cf.
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Numbers%2032:1–42:1&amp;version=nkjv" rel="nofollow">
        Numbers 32:1-42
       </a>
       ), and then describes how Joshua divided the newly conquered land of
       <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
        Canaan
       </a>
       into parcels, and assigned them to the tribes by
       <a href="/wiki/Land_Lottery" title="Land Lottery">
        lot
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Joshua 14:1 also makes reference to the role of
       <a href="/wiki/Eleazar" title="Eleazar">
        Eleazar the priest
       </a>
       (ahead of Joshua) in the distribution process.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The description serves a theological function to show how the
       <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">
        promise of the land
       </a>
       was realized in the biblical narrative; its origins are unclear, but the descriptions may reflect geographical relations among the places named.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         5
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The wording of
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Joshua%2018:1–4:1&amp;version=nkjv" rel="nofollow">
        Joshua 18:1-4
       </a>
       suggests that the tribes of Reuben, Gad,
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Judah" title="Tribe of Judah">
        Judah
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Ephraim" title="Tribe of Ephraim">
        Ephraim
       </a>
       and Manasseh received their land allocation some time before the "remaining seven tribes", and a 21-member expedition set out to survey the remainder of the land with a view to organising the allocation to the tribes of
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Simeon" title="Tribe of Simeon">
        Simeon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Benjamin" title="Tribe of Benjamin">
        Benjamin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Asher" title="Tribe of Asher">
        Asher
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Naphtali" title="Tribe of Naphtali">
        Naphtali
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Zebulun" title="Tribe of Zebulun">
        Zebulun
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Issachar" title="Tribe of Issachar">
        Issachar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Dan" title="Tribe of Dan">
        Dan
       </a>
       . Subsequently, 48
       <a href="/wiki/Levitical_city" title="Levitical city">
        cities
       </a>
       with their surrounding lands were allocated to the
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Levi" title="Tribe of Levi">
        Tribe of Levi
       </a>
       (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Joshua%2021:1–41:1&amp;version=nkjv" rel="nofollow">
        Joshua 21:1-41
       </a>
       , cf.
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Numbers%2035:7–35:7&amp;version=nkjv" rel="nofollow">
        Numbers 35:7
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Omitted in the
       <a href="/wiki/Masoretic_Text" title="Masoretic Text">
        Masoretic Text
       </a>
       , but present in the
       <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">
        Septuagint
       </a>
       , is a statement that:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Joshua completed the division of the land in its boundaries, and the children gave a portion to Joshua, by the commandment of the Lord. They gave to him the city for which he asked,
         <a href="/wiki/Timnath-heres" title="Timnath-heres">
          Thamnath Sarach
         </a>
         gave they him in Mount Ephraim, and Joshua built the city, and dwelt in it. And Joshua took the stone knives with which he had
         <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">
          circumcised
         </a>
         the children of Israel, which were in the way in the wilderness, and he placed them in Tamnath Sarach
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
         <a href="#cite_note-12">
          [12]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       By the end of chapter 21, the narrative records that the fulfilment of God's promise of land, rest and supremacy over the enemies of the Israelites was complete.(Joshua 21:43-45) The writer of the
       <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Hebrews" title="Epistle to the Hebrews">
        Letter to the Hebrews
       </a>
       makes reference to Joshua and the promise of rest in
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews%204:8–4:8&amp;version=nkjv" rel="nofollow">
        Hebrews 4:8
       </a>
       . The tribes to whom Moses had granted land east of the Jordan are authorized to return home to
       <a href="/wiki/Gilead" title="Gilead">
        Gilead
       </a>
       (here used in the widest sense for the whole
       <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_in_the_Bible" title="Transjordan in the Bible">
        Transjordan
       </a>
       district),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       having faithfully 'kept the charge' (Joshua 22:3,
       <a href="/wiki/Revised_Version" title="Revised Version">
        English Revised Version
       </a>
       ) of supporting the tribes occupying Canaan. They are granted "riches ... with very much livestock, with
       <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
        silver
       </a>
       , with
       <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">
        gold
       </a>
       , with
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">
        bronze
       </a>
       , with
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       , and with very much clothing" as a reward (Joshua 22:1-9).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Joshua.27s_farewell_speeches_.28chapters_23.E2.80.9324.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Joshua's_farewell_speeches_(chapters_23–24)">
        Joshua's farewell speeches (chapters 23–24)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Joshua's farewell speeches (chapters 23–24)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Joshua, in his old age and conscious that he is "going the way of all the earth" (Joshua 23:14), gathers the leaders of the Israelites together and reminds them of Yahweh's great works for them, and of the need to love Yahweh (Joshua 23:11). The Israelites are told - just as Joshua himself had been told (Joshua 1:7) - that they must comply with "all that is written in the Book of the Law of Moses" (Joshua 23:6), neither "turn[ing] aside from it to the right hand or to the left" (i.e. by adding to the law, or diminishing from it).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Joshua meets again with all the people at
       <a href="/wiki/Shechem" title="Shechem">
        Shechem
       </a>
       in chapter 24 and addresses them a second time. He recounts the history of God's formation of the Israelite nation, beginning with "
       <a href="/wiki/Terah" title="Terah">
        Terah
       </a>
       , the father of
       <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">
        Abraham
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nahor,_son_of_Terah" title="Nahor, son of Terah">
        Nahor
       </a>
       , [who] lived beyond the
       <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">
        Euphrates River
       </a>
       and worshiped other gods" (
       <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
        <a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Joshua+24%3A2&amp;version=NIV" rel="nofollow">
         Joshua 24:2
        </a>
       </span>
       ). He invited the Israelites to choose between serving the Lord who had delivered them from
       <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
        Egypt
       </a>
       , or the gods which their ancestors had served on the other side of the Euphrates, or the gods of the Amorites in whose land they now lived. The people chose to serve the Lord, a decision which Joshua recorded in the Book of the Law of God. He then erected a memorial stone "under the oak that was by the sanctuary of the Lord" in Shechem (Joshua 24:1-27). The oak is associated with the Oak of
       <a href="/wiki/Moreh" title="Moreh">
        Moreh
       </a>
       where Abram had set up camp during his travels in this area (Genesis 12:6).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Thus "Joshua made a covenant with the people", literally "cut a covenant", a phrase common to the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       languages. It derives from the custom of
       <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">
        sacrifice
       </a>
       , in which the victims were cut in pieces and offered to the
       <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
        deity
       </a>
       invoked in ratification of the engagement.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The people then returned to their inheritance i.e. their allocated lands (Joshua 24:28).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Closing_items">
        Closing items
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Closing items">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Book of Joshua closes with three concluding items (referred to in the Jerusalem Bible as "Two Additions"):
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        The death of Joshua and his burial at Timnath-serah (Joshua 24:29-31)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        The burial of the bones of
        <a href="/wiki/Joseph_(Genesis)" title="Joseph (Genesis)">
         Joseph
        </a>
        at Shechem (Joshua 24:32)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        The death of Eleazar and his burial in land belonging to
        <a href="/wiki/Phinehas" title="Phinehas">
         Phinehas
        </a>
        in the mountains of Ephraim (Joshua 24:33).
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       There were no Levitical cities given to the descendants of
       <a href="/wiki/Aaron" title="Aaron">
        Aaron
       </a>
       in Ephraim, so theologians
       <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Keil" title="Carl Friedrich Keil">
        Carl Friedrich Keil
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Franz_Delitzsch" title="Franz Delitzsch">
        Franz Delitzsch
       </a>
       suppose the land may have been at
       <a href="/wiki/Geba_(city)" title="Geba (city)">
        Geba
       </a>
       in the territory of the
       <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Benjamin" title="Tribe of Benjamin">
        Tribe of Benjamin
       </a>
       : "the situation, 'upon the mountains of Ephraim', is not at variance with this view, as these mountains extended, according to Judges 4:5, etc., far into the territory of Benjamin".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In some manuscripts and editions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">
        Septuagint
       </a>
       , there is an additional verse relating to the apostasy of the Israelites after Joshua's death.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">
        Composition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Composition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="511" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg/220px-Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg/330px-Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg/440px-Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prise_de_J%C3%A9richo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          The Taking of Jericho
         </i>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Jean_Fouquet" title="Jean Fouquet">
          Jean Fouquet
         </a>
         , c.1452–1460)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Book of Joshua is an
       <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_work" title="Anonymous work">
        anonymous work
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Talmud#Babylonian_Talmud" title="Talmud">
        Babylonian Talmud
       </a>
       , written in the 3rd to 5th centuries CE, was the first attempt to attach authors to the
       <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">
        holy books
       </a>
       . Each book, according to the authors of the Talmud, was written by a
       <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">
        prophet
       </a>
       , and each prophet was an
       <a href="/wiki/Eyewitness_testimony" title="Eyewitness testimony">
        eyewitness
       </a>
       of the events described, and
       <a href="/wiki/Joshua" title="Joshua">
        Joshua
       </a>
       himself wrote "the book that bears his name". This idea was rejected as untenable by
       <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">
        John Calvin
       </a>
       (1509–1564), and by the time of
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">
        Thomas Hobbes
       </a>
       (1588–1679) it was recognised that the book must have been written much later than the period it depicted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DePury_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-DePury-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         26–30
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is now general agreement that Joshua was composed as part of a larger work, the
       <a href="/wiki/Deuteronomist#Deuteronomistic_history" title="Deuteronomist">
        Deuteronomistic history
       </a>
       , stretching from the
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deuteronomy" title="Book of Deuteronomy">
        Book of Deuteronomy
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">
        Books of Kings
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younger_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Younger-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         174
        </span>
       </sup>
       In 1943 the German biblical scholar
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Noth" title="Martin Noth">
        Martin Noth
       </a>
       suggested that this history was composed by a single author/editor, living in the time of the Exile (6th century BCE).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Klein-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         317
        </span>
       </sup>
       A major modification to Noth's theory was made in 1973 by the American scholar
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_M._Cross" title="Frank M. Cross">
        Frank M. Cross
       </a>
       , to the effect that two editions of the history could be distinguished, the first and more important from the court of king
       <a href="/wiki/Josiah" title="Josiah">
        Josiah
       </a>
       in the late 7th century BCE, and the second Noth's 6th century BCE Exilic history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DePury_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-DePury-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         63
        </span>
       </sup>
       Later scholars have detected more authors or editors than either Noth or Cross allowed for.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         6
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_and_archaeological_evidence">
        Historical and archaeological evidence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Historical and archaeological evidence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Israel_and_Judah" title="History of ancient Israel and Judah">
        History of ancient Israel and Judah
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The prevailing scholarly view is that Joshua is not a factual account of historical events.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coote_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Coote-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McConville2010_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McConville2010-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         4
        </span>
       </sup>
       The apparent setting of Joshua is the 13th century BCE;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McConville2010_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McConville2010-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       this was a time of widespread city-destruction, but with a few exceptions (
       <a href="/wiki/Tel_Hazor" title="Tel Hazor">
        Hazor
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tel_Lachish" title="Tel Lachish">
        Lachish
       </a>
       ) the destroyed cities are not the ones the Bible associates with Joshua, and the ones it does associate with him show little or no sign of even being occupied at the time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerHayes_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MillerHayes-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         71-72
        </span>
       </sup>
       Given its lack of historicity, Carolyn Pressler in her commentary for the
       <i>
        Westminster Bible Companion
       </i>
       series suggests that readers of Joshua should give priority to its theological message ("what passages teach about God") and be aware of what these would have meant to audiences in the 7th and 6th centuries BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pressler_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pressler-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         5–6
        </span>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_D._Nelson" title="Richard D. Nelson">
        Richard Nelson
       </a>
       explains: The needs of the
       <a href="/wiki/Centralisation" title="Centralisation">
        centralised
       </a>
       monarchy favoured a single story of origins, combining old traditions of an
       <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">
        exodus from Egypt
       </a>
       , belief in a national god as "divine warrior," and explanations for ruined cities,
       <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">
        social stratification
       </a>
       and ethnic groups, and contemporary tribes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nelson-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         5
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In scholarship prior to the early 20th century academic scholarship, the historicity of the early Israelite campaigns was taken for granted (e.g., Paton). However, by the 1930s
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Noth" title="Martin Noth">
        Martin Noth
       </a>
       issued what
       <a href="/wiki/William_F._Albright" title="William F. Albright">
        William F. Albright
       </a>
       termed "a sweeping criticism of the legitimacy of using biblical data in Joshua as material for history."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright1939_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Albright1939-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Noth was a student of
       <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_Alt" title="Albrecht Alt">
        Albrecht Alt
       </a>
       , who emphasized
       <a href="/wiki/Form_criticism" title="Form criticism">
        form criticism
       </a>
       and the importance of
       <a href="/wiki/Origin_myth" title="Origin myth">
        etiology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright1939_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Albright1939-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Alt and Noth posited a peaceful movement of the Israelites into various areas of Canaan,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Citation_signal#Signals_that_indicate_contradiction" title="Citation signal">
         contra
        </a>
       </i>
       the Biblical account.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rendsburg_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rendsburg-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Albright himself questioned the "tenacity" of etiologies, which were key to Noth's analysis of the campaigns in Joshua. Archaeological evidence in the 1930s showed that the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Ai_(Canaan)" title="Ai (Canaan)">
        Ai
       </a>
       , an early target for conquest in the putative Joshua account, had existed and been destroyed, but in the 22nd century BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright1939_25-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Albright1939-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1951
       <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Kenyon" title="Kathleen Kenyon">
        Kathleen Kenyon
       </a>
       showed that Jericho was from the
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age#Age_sub-divisions" title="Bronze Age">
        Middle Bronze Age
       </a>
       (c. 2100–1550 BCE), not the
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age#Age_sub-divisions" title="Bronze Age">
        Late Bronze Age
       </a>
       (c. 1550–1200 BCE). Kenyon argued that the early Israelite campaign could not be historically corroborated, but rather explained as an etiology of the location and a representation of the Israelite settlement.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1955,
       <a href="/wiki/G._Ernest_Wright" title="G. Ernest Wright">
        G. Ernest Wright
       </a>
       discussed the correlation of archaeological data to the early Israelite campaigns, which he divided into three phases per the Book of Joshua. He pointed to two sets of archaeological findings that "seem to suggest that the biblical account is in general correct regarding the nature of the late thirteenth and twelfth-eleventh centuries in the country" (i.e., "a period of tremendous violence").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wright-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He gives particular weight to what were then recent digs at Hazor by
       <a href="/wiki/Yigael_Yadin" title="Yigael Yadin">
        Yigael Yadin
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wright-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As an alternative to both the military conquest and uncontested infiltration hypotheses,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Mendenhall" title="George Mendenhall">
        George Mendenhall
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Gottwald" title="Norman Gottwald">
        Norman Gottwald
       </a>
       suggested that the Israelites emerged through a kind of
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_peasant_revolts" title="List of peasant revolts">
        peasant revolt
       </a>
       against their Canaanite lords. However, as explained by
       <a href="/wiki/Gary_A._Rendsburg" title="Gary A. Rendsburg">
        Gary A. Rendsburg
       </a>
       , archaeological findings (presented by
       <a href="/wiki/Israel_Finkelstein" title="Israel Finkelstein">
        Israel Finkelstein
       </a>
       in 1986) undermined this idea because the Israelites' first settled areas were not held by Canaanites, whose cities were sustained alongside Israelite areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rendsburg_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rendsburg-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mainstream
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">
        Ancient Near East
       </a>
       scholarship holds that the Book of Joshua holds little historical value.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Killebrew_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Killebrew-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         152
        </span>
       </sup>
       The archaeological evidence shows that
       <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">
        Jericho
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ai_(Canaan)" title="Ai (Canaan)">
        Ai
       </a>
       were not occupied in the Near Eastern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age" title="Late Bronze Age">
        Late Bronze Age
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The story of the conquest most likely represents the nationalist
       <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">
        propaganda
       </a>
       of the 8th century BCE kings of
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
        Judah
       </a>
       and their claims to the territory of the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">
        Kingdom of Israel
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coote_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Coote-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       incorporated into an early form of Joshua written late in the reign of king
       <a href="/wiki/Josiah" title="Josiah">
        Josiah
       </a>
       (reigned 640–609 BCE). The book was probably revised and completed after the
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(587_BC)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (587 BC)">
        fall of Jerusalem
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">
        Neo-Babylonian Empire
       </a>
       in 586 BCE, and possibly after the return from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_exile" title="Babylonian exile">
        Babylonian exile
       </a>
       in 538 BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Creach_4-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Creach-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         10-11
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Themes">
        Themes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Themes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3353" data-file-width="5103" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg/350px-JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg/525px-JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg/700px-JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JoshuaSun_Martin.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Joshua Commanding the Sun to Stand Still upon Gideon
         </i>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/John_Martin_(painter)" title="John Martin (painter)">
          John Martin
         </a>
         )
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The overarching theological theme of the Deuteronomistic history is faithfulness and God's mercy, and their opposites, faithlessness and God's wrath. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Judges" title="Book of Judges">
        Book of Judges
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Samuel" title="Books of Samuel">
        Books of Samuel
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">
        Books of Kings
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
        Israelites
       </a>
       become faithless and God ultimately shows his anger by sending his people into exile.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       But in Joshua Israel is obedient, Joshua is faithful, and God fulfills his promise and gives them the land.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pressler_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pressler-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         3–4
        </span>
       </sup>
       Yahweh's war campaign in
       <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
        Canaan
       </a>
       validates Israel's entitlement to the land
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McConville2001_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McConville2001-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         158-159
        </span>
       </sup>
       and provides a paradigm of how Israel was to live there: twelve tribes, with a designated leader, united by
       <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(historical)" title="Covenant (historical)">
        covenant
       </a>
       in warfare and in worship of Yahweh alone at a single sanctuary, all in obedience to the commands of
       <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
        Moses
       </a>
       as found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deuteronomy" title="Book of Deuteronomy">
        Book of Deuteronomy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         162
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="God_and_Israel">
        God and Israel
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: God and Israel">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Book of Joshua takes forward Deuteronomy's theme of Israel as a single people worshipping
       <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">
        Yahweh
       </a>
       in the land God has given them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McConville2001_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McConville2001-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         159
        </span>
       </sup>
       Yahweh, as the main character in the book, takes the initiative in conquering the land, and Yahweh's power wins the battles. For example, the walls of
       <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">
        Jericho
       </a>
       fall because Yahweh fights for Israel, not because the Israelites show superior fighting ability.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Creach_4-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Creach-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         7-8
        </span>
       </sup>
       The potential disunity of Israel is a constant theme, the greatest threat of disunity coming from the tribes east of the Jordan. Chapter 22:19 even hints that the land across the Jordan is unclean and that the tribes who live there have secondary status.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Creach_4-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Creach-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         9
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Land">
        Land
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Land">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Land is the central topic of Joshua.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McConville2010_21-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-McConville2010-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         11
        </span>
       </sup>
       The introduction to Deuteronomy recalled how Yahweh had given the land to the Israelites but then withdrew the gift when Israel showed fear and only Joshua and
       <a href="/wiki/Caleb" title="Caleb">
        Caleb
       </a>
       had trusted in God.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerDeut_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MillerDeut-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         33
        </span>
       </sup>
       The land is Yahweh's to give or withhold, and the fact that he has promised it to Israel gives Israel an inalienable right to take it. For
       <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">
        exilic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_period" title="Second Temple period">
        post-exilic
       </a>
       readers, the land was both the sign of Yahweh's faithfulness and Israel's unfaithfulness, as well as the centre of their ethnic identity. In Deuteronomistic theology, "rest" meant Israel's unthreatened possession of the land, the achievement of which began with the conquests of Joshua.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nelson-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         15–16
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_enemy">
        The enemy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The enemy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Joshua "carries out a systematic campaign against the civilians of Canaan — men, women and children — that amounts to
       <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">
        genocide
       </a>
       ."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In doing this he is carrying out
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Herem_(war_or_property)" title="Herem (war or property)">
         herem
        </a>
       </i>
       as commanded by Yahweh in Deuteronomy 20:17: "You shall not leave alive anything that breathes". The purpose is to drive out and dispossess the Canaanites, with the implication that there are to be no
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">
        treaties
       </a>
       with the enemy, no
       <a href="/wiki/Mercy" title="Mercy">
        mercy
       </a>
       , and no
       <a href="/wiki/Exogamy" title="Exogamy">
        intermarriage
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younger_6-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Younger-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         175
        </span>
       </sup>
       "The extermination of the nations glorifies Yahweh as a warrior and promotes Israel's claim to the land," while their continued survival "explores the themes of disobedience and penalty and looks forward to the story told in Judges and Kings."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nelson-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         18–19
        </span>
       </sup>
       The divine call for massacre at
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jericho" title="Battle of Jericho">
        Jericho
       </a>
       and elsewhere can be explained in terms of cultural norms (Israel was not the only
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
        Iron Age
       </a>
       state to practice
       <i>
        herem
       </i>
       ) and theology (a measure to ensure Israel's purity as well as the fulfillment of God's promise),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younger_6-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Younger-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         175
        </span>
       </sup>
       but
       <a href="/wiki/Patrick_D._Miller" title="Patrick D. Miller">
        Patrick D. Miller
       </a>
       in his commentary on Deuteronomy remarks, "there is no real way to make such reports palatable to the hearts and minds of contemporary readers and believers."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerDeut_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-MillerDeut-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         40–41
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Obedience">
        Obedience
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Obedience">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Obedience versus disobedience is a constant theme of the work.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         79
        </span>
       </sup>
       Obedience ties in the Jordan crossing, the defeat of Jericho and
       <a href="/wiki/Ai_(Canaan)" title="Ai (Canaan)">
        Ai
       </a>
       , circumcision and Passover, and the public display and reading of the Law. Disobedience appears in the story of
       <a href="/wiki/Achan_(biblical_figure)" title="Achan (biblical figure)">
        Achan
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Stoning" title="Stoning">
        stoned
       </a>
       for violating the
       <i>
        herem
       </i>
       command), the
       <a href="/wiki/Gibeon_(ancient_city)" title="Gibeon (ancient city)">
        Gibeonites
       </a>
       , and the altar built by the Transjordan tribes. Joshua's two final addresses challenge the Israel of the future (the readers of the story) to obey the most important command of all, to worship Yahweh and no other gods. Joshua thus illustrates the central Deuteronomistic message, that obedience leads to success and disobedience to ruin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_24-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nelson-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         20
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Moses.2C_Joshua_and_Josiah">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Moses,_Joshua_and_Josiah">
        Moses, Joshua and Josiah
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Moses, Joshua and Josiah">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Deuteronomistic history draws parallels in proper leadership between
       <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
        Moses
       </a>
       , Joshua and
       <a href="/wiki/Josiah" title="Josiah">
        Josiah
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_24-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nelson-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         102
        </span>
       </sup>
       God's commission to Joshua in chapter 1 is framed as a royal installation. The people's pledge of loyalty to Joshua as the successor of Moses recalls royal practices. The covenant-renewal ceremony led by Joshua was the prerogative of the kings of Judah. God's command to Joshua to meditate on the "book of the law" day and night parallels the description of Josiah in 2 Kings 23:25 as a king uniquely concerned with the study of the law. The two figures had identical territorial goals; Josiah died in 609 BCE while attempting to annex the former Israel to his own kingdom of Judah.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some of the parallels with Moses can be seen in the following, and not exhaustive, list:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younger_6-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Younger-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         174
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Joshua sent
        <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">
         spies
        </a>
        to scout out the land near Jericho (2:1), just as Moses sent spies from the wilderness to scout out the
        <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">
         Promised Land
        </a>
        (Num. 13; Deut. 1:19–25).
       </li>
       <li>
        Joshua led the Israelites out of the wilderness into the Promised Land, crossing the Jordan River as if on dry ground (3:16), just as Moses led the Israelites out of Egypt through the
        <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">
         Red Sea
        </a>
        , which they crossed as if on dry land (Ex. 14:22).
       </li>
       <li>
        After crossing the
        <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">
         Jordan River
        </a>
        , the Israelites celebrated the
        <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">
         Passover
        </a>
        (5:10–12) just as they did immediately before
        <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">
         the Exodus
        </a>
        (Exodus 12).
       </li>
       <li>
        Joshua's vision of the "commander of Yahweh's army" (5:13–15) is reminiscent of the divine revelation to Moses in the
        <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">
         burning bush
        </a>
        (Ex. 3:1–6).
       </li>
       <li>
        Joshua successfully intercedes on behalf of the Israelites when Yahweh is angry for their failure to fully observe the "ban" (
        <a href="/wiki/Herem_(war_or_property)" title="Herem (war or property)">
         herem
        </a>
        ), just as Moses frequently persuaded God not to punish the people (Ex. 32:11–14, Num. 11:2, 14:13–19).
       </li>
       <li>
        Joshua and the Israelites were able to defeat the people at Ai because Joshua followed the divine instruction to extend his sword (Josh 8:18), just as the people were able to defeat the
        <a href="/wiki/Amalek#Amalekites_in_the_Hebrew_Bible" title="Amalek">
         Amalekites
        </a>
        as long as Moses extended his hand that held the
        <a href="/wiki/Staff_of_Moses" title="Staff of Moses">
         staff of God
        </a>
        (Ex. 17:8–13).
       </li>
       <li>
        Joshua is "old, advanced in years (13:1) at the time when the Israelites can begin to settle on the promised land, just as Moses was old when he died having seen, but not entered, the Promised Land (Deuteronomy 34:7)
       </li>
       <li>
        Joshua served as the mediator of the renewed covenant between Yahweh and Israel at Shechem (8:30–35; 24), just as Moses was the mediator of Yahweh's covenant with the people at
        <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai" title="Mount Sinai">
         Mount Sinai
        </a>
        /
