        <a href="/wiki/Mount_Horeb" title="Mount Horeb">
         Mount Horeb
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Before his death, Joshua delivered a farewell address to the Israelites (23–24), just as Moses had delivered his farewell address (Deuteronomy 32–33).
       </li>
       <li>
        Moses lived to be 120 (Deuteronomy 34:7) and Joshua lived to be 110 (Joshua 24:29).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Moral_and_political_interpretations">
        Moral and political interpretations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_Joshua&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Moral and political interpretations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       With the
       <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">
        Zionist
       </a>
       struggle for a
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_state" title="Jewish state">
        Jewish state
       </a>
       , the early Israelite campaigns have undergone renewed attention and interpretation. The early Zionists, according to
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rachel_Havrelock&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rachel Havrelock (page does not exist)">
        Rachel Havrelock
       </a>
       , "read the book of Joshua as explaining their times and justifying their wars. From this perspective, God fought on behalf of manifest Israel.... Joshua’s vocabulary informed the lexicon of Jewish nationalism."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Havrelock_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Havrelock-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Later, in 1958,
       <a href="/wiki/David_Ben-Gurion" title="David Ben-Gurion">
        David Ben-Gurion
       </a>
       "saw the biblical war narrative as constituting an ideal basis for a unifying
       <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">
        myth
       </a>
       of national identity." This was a unity that was framed against a common enemy, the
       <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">
        Arabs
       </a>
       beyond Israel's borders.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Havrelock_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Havrelock-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ben-Gurion met with politicians and scholars, such as Bible scholar
       <a href="/wiki/Shemaryahu_Talmon" title="Shemaryahu Talmon">
        Shemaryahu Talmon
       </a>
       , to discuss the conquests in Joshua. He later published a book of the meeting transcripts. In a lecture at Ben-Gurion's home, archaeologist
       <a href="/wiki/Yigael_Yadin" title="Yigael Yadin">
        Yigael Yadin
       </a>
       argued for the historicity of the Israelite military campaign and remarked on how much easier it was for military experts to appreciate the plausibility of the Joshua narrative. Yadin specifically pointed to the conquests of
       <a href="/wiki/Tel_Hazor" title="Tel Hazor">
        Hazor
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bethel" title="Bethel">
        Bethel
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lachish" title="Lachish">
        Lachish
       </a>
       . Conversely, archaeologist
       <a href="/wiki/Yohanan_Aharoni" title="Yohanan Aharoni">
        Yohanan Aharoni
       </a>
       argued against the historicity of the early Israelite campaigns, instead favoring a migration model.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Havrelock_39-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Havrelock-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Rachel Havrelock herself argues that the myth of conquest, although shaped by ardent nationalists with a military agenda, could be re-interpreted for the purposes of a decentralized post-nationalism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Havrelock_39-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Havrelock-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By the same token, the Biblical narrative of conquest has been used as an apparatus of critique against Zionism. For example, Michael Prior criticizes the use of the campaign in Joshua to favor "colonial enterprises" (in general, not only Zionism) and have been interpreted as validating
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">
        ethnic cleansing
       </a>
       . He asserts that the Bible was used to make the treatment of
       <a href="/wiki/Palestinians" title="Palestinians">
        Palestinians
       </a>
       more palatable morally.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A related moral condemnation can be seen in "The political sacralization of imperial genocide: contextualizing Timothy Dwight's
       <i>
        The Conquest of Canaan
       </i>
       " by Bill Templer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This kind of critique is not new;
       <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Boyarin" title="Jonathan Boyarin">
        Jonathan Boyarin
       </a>
       notes how
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_W._Turner" title="Frederick W. Turner">
        Frederick W. Turner
       </a>
       blamed Israel's
       <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">
        monotheism
       </a>
       for the very idea of genocide, which Boyarin found "simplistic" yet with precedents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

        translations:
